   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Headache
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

      <tr class="noprint">
       <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
        <div style="text-align: right;">
         [
         <a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q86" title="d:Q86">
          edit on Wikidata
         </a>

     <p>
      <b>
       Headache
      </b>
      is the symptom of
      <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">
       pain
      </a>
      anywhere in the region of the
      <a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">
       head
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">
       neck
      </a>
      . It occurs in
      <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">
       migraines
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension-type_headache" title="Tension-type headache">
       tension-type headaches
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Cluster_headache" title="Cluster headache">
       cluster headaches
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Frequent headaches can affect relationships and employment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is also an increased risk of depression in those with severe headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Headaches can occur as a result of many conditions whether serious or not. There are a number of different classification systems for headaches. The most well-recognized is that of the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Headache_Society" title="International Headache Society">
       International Headache Society
      </a>
      . Causes of headaches may include fatigue, sleep deprivation, stress, the effects of medications, the effects of recreational drugs, viral infections, common colds, head injury, rapid ingestion of a very cold food or beverage, and dental or sinus issues.
     </p>
     <p>
      Treatment of a headache depends on the underlying cause, but commonly involves
      <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">
       pain medication
      </a>
      . Some form of headache is one of the most commonly experienced of all physical discomforts.
     </p>
     <p>
      About half of adults have a headache in a given year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tension headaches are the most common affecting about 1.6 billion people (21.8% of the population) followed by migraine headaches which affect about 848 million (11.7%).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">
       Cause
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Cause">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      There are more than 200 types of headaches. Some are harmless and some are life-threatening. The description of the headache and findings on
      <a href="/wiki/Neurological_examination" title="Neurological examination">
       neurological examination
      </a>
      , determine whether additional tests are needed and what treatment is best.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIGN_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SIGN-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_vs._secondary_headaches">
       Primary vs. secondary headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Primary vs. secondary headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Headaches are broadly classified as "primary" or "secondary".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Primary headaches are benign, recurrent headaches not caused by underlying disease or structural problems. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">
       migraine
      </a>
      is a type of primary headache. While primary headaches may cause significant daily pain and disability, they are not dangerous. Secondary headaches are caused by an underlying disease, like an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systemic_infection" title="Systemic infection">
       infection
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Head_injury" title="Head injury">
       head injury
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_disorders" title="Vascular disorders">
       vascular disorders
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_hemorrhage" title="Subarachnoid hemorrhage">
       brain bleed
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">
       tumors
      </a>
      . Secondary headaches can be harmless or dangerous. Certain "red flags" or warning signs indicate a secondary headache may be dangerous.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_headaches">
       Primary headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Primary headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      90% of all headaches are primary headaches. Primary headaches usually first start when people are between 20 and 40 years old .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common types of primary headaches are migraines and tension-type headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      They have different characteristics. Migraines typically present with pulsing head pain, nausea, photophobia (sensitivity to light) and phonophobia (sensitivity to sound). Tension-type headaches usually present with non-pulsing "bandlike" pressure on both sides of the head, not accompanied by other symptoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detsky_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Detsky-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other very rare types of primary headaches include:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_headaches" title="Cluster headaches">
        cluster headaches
       </a>
       : short episodes (15–180 minutes) of severe pain, usually around one eye, with autonomic symptoms (tearing, red eye, nasal congestion) which occur at the same time every day. Cluster headaches can be treated with triptans and prevented with prednisone, ergotamine or lithium.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_neuralgia" title="Trigeminal neuralgia">
        trigeminal neuralgia
       </a>
       : shooting face pain
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Hemicrania_continua" title="Hemicrania continua">
        hemicrania continua
       </a>
       : continuous unilateral pain with episodes of severe pain. Hemicrania continua can be relieved by the medication indomethacin.
      </li>
      <li>
       primary stabbing headache: recurrent episodes of stabbing "ice pick pain" or "jabs and jolts" for 1 second to several minutes without autonomic symptoms (tearing, red eye, nasal congestion). These headaches can be treated with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indomethacin" title="Indomethacin">
        indomethacin
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       primary cough headache: starts suddenly and lasts for several minutes after coughing, sneezing or straining (anything that may increase pressure in the head). Serious etiologies(see secondary headaches red flag section) must be ruled out before a diagnosis of "benign" primary cough headache can be made.
      </li>
      <li>
       primary exertional headache: throbbing, pulsatile pain which starts during or after exercising, lasting for 5 minutes to 24 hours. The mechanism behind these headaches is unclear, possibly due to straining causing veins in the head to dilate, causing pain. These headaches can be prevented by not exercising too strenuously and can be treated with medications such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indomethacin" title="Indomethacin">
        indomethacin
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Sexual_headache" title="Sexual headache">
        primary sex headache
       </a>
       : dull, bilateral headache that starts during sexual activity and becomes much worse during orgasm. These headaches are thought to be due to lower pressure in the head during sex. It is important to realize that headaches that begin during orgasm may be due to a subarachnoid hemorrhage, so serious causes must be ruled out first. These headaches are treated by advising the person to stop sex if they develop a headache. Medications such as
       <a href="/wiki/Propranolol" title="Propranolol">
        propranolol
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Diltiazem" title="Diltiazem">
        diltiazem
       </a>
       can also be helpful.
      </li>
      <li>
       hypnic headache: moderate-severe headache that starts a few hours after falling asleep and lasts 15–30 minutes. The headache may recur several times during night. Hypnic headaches are usually in older women. They may be treated with
       <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">
        lithium
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary_headaches">
       Secondary headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Secondary headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Headaches may be caused by problems elsewhere in the head or neck. Some of these are not harmful, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Cervicogenic_headache" title="Cervicogenic headache">
       cervicogenic headache
      </a>
      (pain arising from the neck muscles).
      <a href="/wiki/Medication_overuse_headache" title="Medication overuse headache">
       Medication overuse headache
      </a>
      may occur in those using excessive painkillers for headaches, paradoxically causing worsening headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIGN_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SIGN-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More serious causes of secondary headaches include:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
        meningitis
       </a>
       : inflammation of the meninges which presents with fever and meningismus, or stiff neck
      </li>
      <li>
       bleeding inside the brain (
       <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_hemorrhage" title="Intracranial hemorrhage">
        intracranial hemorrhage
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_hemorrhage" title="Subarachnoid hemorrhage">
        subarachnoid hemorrhage
       </a>
       (acute, severe headache, stiff neck WITHOUT fever)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruptured_aneurysm" title="Ruptured aneurysm">
        ruptured aneurysm
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Arteriovenous_malformation" title="Arteriovenous malformation">
        arteriovenous malformation
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Intraparenchymal_hemorrhage" title="Intraparenchymal hemorrhage">
        intraparenchymal hemorrhage
       </a>
       (headache only)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">
        brain tumor
       </a>
       : dull headache, worse with exertion and change in position, accompanied by nausea and vomiting. Often, the person will have nausea and vomiting for weeks before the headache starts.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_arteritis" title="Temporal arteritis">
        temporal arteritis
       </a>
       : inflammatory disease of arteries common in the elderly (average age 70) with fever, headache, weight loss, jaw claudication, tender vessels by the temples, polymyalgia rheumatica
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma" title="Glaucoma">
        acute closed angle glaucoma
       </a>
       (increased pressure in the eyeball): headache that starts with eye pain, blurry vision, associated with nausea and vomiting. On physical exam, the person will have a red eye and a fixed, mid dilated pupil.
      </li>
      <li>
       Post-ictal headaches: Headaches that happen after a convulsion or other type of seizure, as part of the period after the seizure (the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-ictal" title="Post-ictal">
        post-ictal
       </a>
       state)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">
       Pathophysiology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">
       brain
      </a>
      itself is not sensitive to
      <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">
       pain
      </a>
      , because it lacks
      <a href="/wiki/Nociceptor" title="Nociceptor">
       pain receptors
      </a>
      . However, several areas of the
      <a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">
       head
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">
       neck
      </a>
      do have pain receptors and can thus sense pain. These include the extracranial arteries,
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_meningeal_artery" title="Middle meningeal artery">
       middle meningeal artery
      </a>
      , large veins,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venous_sinuses" title="Venous sinuses">
       venous sinuses
      </a>
      , cranial and spinal nerves, head and neck muscles, the
      <a href="/wiki/Meninges" title="Meninges">
       meninges
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Falx_cerebri" title="Falx cerebri">
       falx cerebri
      </a>
      , parts of the brainstem, eyes, ears, teeth and lining of the mouth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACEP2008_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ACEP2008-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinical_Neurology_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinical_Neurology-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pial arteries, rather than pial veins are responsible for pain production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Headaches often result from traction to or irritation of the meninges and blood vessels. The nociceptors may be stimulated by head trauma or tumors and cause headaches. Blood vessel spasms, dilated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_vessels" title="Blood vessels">
       blood vessels
      </a>
      , inflammation or infection of meninges and muscular tension can also stimulate nociceptors and cause pain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinical_Neurology_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinical_Neurology-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Once stimulated, a nociceptor sends a message up the length of the nerve fiber to the nerve cells in the brain, signaling that a part of the body hurts.
     </p>
     <p>
      Primary headaches are more difficult to understand than secondary headaches. The exact mechanisms which cause migraines, tension headaches and cluster headaches are not known. There have been different theories over time which attempt to explain what happens in the brain to cause these headaches.
     </p>
     <p>
      Migraines are currently thought to be caused by dysfunction of the nerves in the brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uptodate_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-uptodate-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Previously, migraines were thought to be caused by a primary problem with the blood vessels in the brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This vascular theory, which was developed in the 20th century by Wolff, suggested that the aura in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migraines" title="Migraines">
       migraines
      </a>
      is caused by constriction of intracranial vessels (vessels inside the brain), and the headache itself is caused by rebound dilation of extracranial vessels (vessels just outside the brain). Dilation of these extracranial blood vessels activates the pain receptors in the surrounding nerves, causing a headache. The vascular theory is no longer accepted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uptodate_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-uptodate-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charles_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Charles-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies have shown migraine head pain is not accompanied by extracranial vasodilation, but rather only has some mild intracranial vasodilation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amin_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Amin-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Currently, most specialists think migraines are due to a primary problem with the nerves in the brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uptodate_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-uptodate-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Auras are thought to be caused by a wave of increased activity of neurons in the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">
       cerebral cortex
      </a>
      (a part of the brain) known as cortical spreading depression
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      followed by a period of depressed activity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some people think headaches are caused by the activation of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_nerves" title="Sensory nerves">
       sensory nerves
      </a>
      which release peptides, such as serotonin, causing inflammation in arteries, dura and meninges and also cause some vasodilation. Triptans, medications which treat migraines, block serotonin receptors and constrict blood vessels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      People who are more susceptible to experience migraines without headache are those who have a family history of migraines, women, and women who are experiencing hormonal changes or are taking
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_control_pills" title="Birth control pills">
       birth control pills
      </a>
      or are prescribed
      <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy_(menopause)" title="Hormone replacement therapy (menopause)">
       hormone replacement therapy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_headaches" title="Tension headaches">
       Tension headaches
      </a>
      are thought to be caused by activation of peripheral nerves in the head and neck muscles
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loder_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Loder-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_headaches" title="Cluster headaches">
       Cluster headaches
      </a>
      involve overactivation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_nerve" title="Trigeminal nerve">
       trigeminal nerve
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">
       hypothalamus
      </a>
      in the brain, but the exact cause is unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leroux_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leroux-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">
       Diagnosis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table border="1" class="wikitable">
      <caption>
       Differential diagnosis of headaches
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/Tension_headache" title="Tension headache">
         Tension headache
        </a>
       </th>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/New_daily_persistent_headache" title="New daily persistent headache">
         New daily persistent headache
        </a>
       </th>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/Cluster_headache" title="Cluster headache">
         Cluster headache
        </a>
       </th>
       <th>
        <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">
         Migraine
        </a>
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>

       </td>

       </td>

       </td>

       </td>

        </a>
        or noise sensitivity
       </td>
       <td>
       </td>
       <td>
        may be accompanied by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Running_nose" title="Running nose">
