        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Tears" title="Tears">
         tears
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Ptosis_(eyelid)" title="Ptosis (eyelid)">
         drooping eyelid
        </a>
        , often only on one side
       </td>
       <td>
        sensitivity to movement, light, and noise
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
       </td>
       <td>
        exacerbated by regular use of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetaminophen" title="Acetaminophen">
         acetaminophen
        </a>
        or
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDS" title="NSAIDS">
         NSAIDS
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
       </td>
       <td>
        may exist with tension headache
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBDtriptans_20-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-BBDtriptans-20">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Most headaches can be diagnosed by the clinical history alone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If the symptoms described by the person sound dangerous, further testing with neuroimaging and/or lumbar puncture may be necessary. Electroencephalography (EEG) is not useful for headache diagnosis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first step to diagnosing a headache is to determine if the headache is old or new.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A "new headache" can be a headache that has started recently, or a chronic headache that has changed character.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, if a person has chronic weekly headaches with pressure on both sides of his head, and then develops a sudden severe throbbing headache on one side of his head, he has a new headache.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Red_flags">
       Red flags
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Red flags">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      It can be challenging to differentiate between low-risk, benign headaches and high-risk, dangerous headaches since symptoms are often similar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abrams_Journal_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Abrams_Journal-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Headaches that are possibly dangerous require further lab tests and imaging to diagnose.
     </p>
     <p>
      The American College for Emergency Physicians published criteria for low-risk headaches. They are as follows:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACEP_Criteria_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ACEP_Criteria-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       age younger than 30 years
      </li>
      <li>
       features typical of primary headache
      </li>
      <li>
       history of similar headache
      </li>
      <li>
       no abnormal findings on neurologic exam
      </li>
      <li>
       no concerning change in normal headache pattern
      </li>
      <li>
       no high-risk comorbid conditions (for example, HIV)
      </li>
      <li>
       no new concerning history or physical examination findings
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      A number of characteristics make it more likely that the headache is due to potentially dangerous secondary causes which may be life-threatening or cause long-term damage. These "red flag" symptoms means that a headache warrants further investigation with neuroimaging and lab tests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In general, people complaining of their "first" or "worst" headache warrant imaging and further workup.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      People with progressively worsening headache also warrant imaging, as they may have a mass or a bleed that is gradually growing, pressing on surrounding structures and causing worsening pain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abrams_Journal_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Abrams_Journal-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      People with neurological findings on exam, such as weakness, also need further workup.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abrams_Journal_23-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Abrams_Journal-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The American Headache Society recommends using "SSNOOP", a mnemonic to remember the red flags for identifying a secondary headache:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_22-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Systemic symptoms (fever or weight loss)
      </li>
      <li>
       Systemic disease (HIV infection, malignancy)
      </li>
      <li>
       Neurologic symptoms or signs
      </li>
      <li>
       Onset sudden (thunderclap headache)
      </li>
      <li>
       Onset after age 40 years
      </li>
      <li>
       Previous headache history (first, worst, or different headache)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Other red flag symptoms include:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_22-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abrams_Journal_23-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Abrams_Journal-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hainer_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hainer-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Red Flag
       </th>
       <th>
        Possible causes
       </th>
       <th>
        Reason why red flag indicates possible causes
       </th>
       <th>
        Diagnostic tests
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        New headache after age 50
       </td>
       <td>
        Temporal arteritis, mass in brain
       </td>
       <td>
        Temporal arteritis is an inflammation of vessels close to the temples in older people, which decreases blood flow to the brain and causes pain. May also have tenderness in temples and/or jaw claudication. Some brain cancers are more common in older people.
       </td>
       <td>
        Erythrocyte sedimentation rate (diagnostic test for temporal arteritis), neuroimaging
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Very sudden onset headache (
        <a href="/wiki/Thunderclap_headache" title="Thunderclap headache">
         thunderclap headache
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        Brain bleed (
        <a href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_hemorrhage" title="Subarachnoid hemorrhage">
         subarachnoid hemorrhage
        </a>
        , hemorrhage into mass lesion,
        <a href="/wiki/Vascular_malformation" title="Vascular malformation">
         vascular malformation
        </a>
        ),
        <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_apoplexy" title="Pituitary apoplexy">
         pituitary apoplexy
        </a>
        , mass (especially in
        <a href="/wiki/Posterior_cranial_fossa" title="Posterior cranial fossa">
         posterior fossa
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        A bleed in the brain irritates the meninges which causes pain. Pituitary apoplexy (bleeding or impaired blood supply to the pituitary gland at the base of the brain) is often accompanied by double vision or visual field defects, since the pituitary gland is right next to the
        <a href="/wiki/Optic_chiasm" title="Optic chiasm">
         optic chiasm
        </a>
        (eye nerves).
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Neuroimaging" title="Neuroimaging">
         Neuroimaging
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_puncture" title="Lumbar puncture">
         lumbar puncture
        </a>
        if computed tomography is negative
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headaches increasing in frequency and severity
       </td>
       <td>
        Mass, subdural hematoma, medication overuse
       </td>
       <td>
        As a brain mass gets larger, or a
        <a href="/wiki/Subdural_hematoma" title="Subdural hematoma">
         subdural hematoma
        </a>
        (blood outside the vessels underneath the
        <a href="/wiki/Dura_mater" title="Dura mater">
         dura
        </a>
        ) it pushes more on surrounding structures causing pain. Medication overuse headaches worsen with more medication taken over time.
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, drug screen
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        New onset headache in a person with possible HIV or cancer
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
         Meningitis
        </a>
        (chronic or carcinomatous),
        <a href="/wiki/Brain_abscess" title="Brain abscess">
         brain abscess
        </a>
        including
        <a href="/wiki/Toxoplasmosis" title="Toxoplasmosis">
         toxoplasmosis
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">
         metastasis
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        People with HIV or cancer are immunosuppressed so are likely to get infections of the meninges or infections in the brain causing abscesses. Cancer can metastasize, or travel through the blood or lymph to other sites in the body.
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, lumbar puncture if neuroimaging is negative
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headache with signs of total body illness (fever, stiff neck, rash)
       </td>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
         Meningitis
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">
         encephalitis
        </a>
        (inflammation of the brain tissue),
        <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease" title="Lyme disease">
         Lyme disease
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collagen_vascular_disease" title="Collagen vascular disease">
         collagen vascular disease
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        A stiff neck, or inability to flex the neck due to pain, indicates inflammation of the meninges. Other signs of systemic illness indicates infection.
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, lumbar puncture, serology (diagnostic blood tests for infections)
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Papilledema" title="Papilledema">
         Papilledema
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        brain mass,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benign_intracranial_hypertension" title="Benign intracranial hypertension">
         benign intracranial hypertension
        </a>
        (pseudotumor cerebri),
        <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
         meningitis
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        Increased intracranial pressure pushes on the eyes (from inside the brain) and causes papilledema.
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, lumbar puncture
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Severe headache following head trauma
       </td>
       <td>
        Brain bleeds (
        <a href="/wiki/Intracranial_hemorrhage" title="Intracranial hemorrhage">
         intracranial hemorrhage
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Subdural_hematoma" title="Subdural hematoma">
         subdural hematoma
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Epidural_hematoma" title="Epidural hematoma">
         epidural hematoma
        </a>
        ), post-traumatic headache
       </td>
       <td>
        Trauma can cause bleeding in the brain or shake the nerves, causing a post-traumatic headache
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging of brain, skull, and possibly cervical spine
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Inability to move a limb
       </td>
       <td>
        Arteriovenous malformation, collagen vascular disease, intracranial mass lesion
       </td>
       <td>
        Focal neurological signs indicate something is pushing against nerves in the brain responsible for one part of the body
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, blood tests for collagen vascular diseases
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Change in personality, consciousness, or mental status
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system_infection" title="Central nervous system infection">
         Central nervous system infection
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intracranial_bleeding" title="Intracranial bleeding">
         intracranial bleed
        </a>
        , mass
       </td>
       <td>
        Change in mental status indicates a global infection or inflammation of the brain, or a large bleed compressing the brainstem where the consciousness centers lie
       </td>
       <td>
        Blood tests, lumbar puncture, neuroimaging
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headache triggered by cough, exertion or while engaged in sexual intercourse
       </td>
       <td>
        Mass lesion, subarachnoid hemorrhage
       </td>
       <td>
        Coughing and exertion increases the intra cranial pressure, which may cause a vessel to burst, causing a subarachnoid hemorrhage. A mass lesion already increases intracranial pressure, so an additional increase in intracranial pressure from coughing etc. will cause pain.
       </td>
       <td>
        Neuroimaging, lumbar puncture
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_headaches">
       Old headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Old headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Old headaches are usually primary headaches and are not dangerous. They are most often caused by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migraines" title="Migraines">
       migraines
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_headaches" title="Tension headaches">
       tension headaches
      </a>
      . Migraines are often unilateral, pulsing headaches accompanied by nausea and/or vomiting. There may be an aura (visual symptoms, numbness or tingling) 30–60 minutes before the headache, warning the person of a headache. Migraines may also not have auras.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hainer_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hainer-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tension type headaches usually have bilateral "bandlike" pressure on both sides of the head usually without nausea or vomiting. However, some symptoms from both headache groups may overlap. It is important to distinguish between the two because the treatments are different.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hainer_25-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hainer-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The mnemonic 'POUND' helps distinguish between migraines and tension type headaches.
      <b>
       POUND
      </b>
      stands for
      <b>
       P
      </b>
      ulsatile quality, 4–72 h
      <b>
       O
      </b>
      urs in length,
      <b>
       U
      </b>
      nilateral location,
      <b>
       N
      </b>
      ausea or vomiting,
      <b>
       D
      </b>
      isabling intensity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detsky_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Detsky-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      One review article found that if 4–5 of the POUND characteristics are present, migraine is 24 times as likely a diagnosis than tension type headache (
      <a href="/wiki/Likelihood_ratios_in_diagnostic_testing" title="Likelihood ratios in diagnostic testing">
       likelihood ratio
      </a>
      24). If 3 characteristics of POUND are present, migraine is 3 times more likely a diagnosis than tension type headache (
      <a href="/wiki/Likelihood_ratios_in_diagnostic_testing" title="Likelihood ratios in diagnostic testing">
       likelihood ratio
      </a>
      3).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Detsky_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Detsky-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If only 2 POUND characteristics are present, tension type headaches are 60% more likely (likelihood ratio 0.41). Another study found the following factors independently each increase the chance of migraine over tension type headache: nausea, photophobia, phonophobia, exacerbation by physical activity, unilateral, throbbing quality, chocolate as headache trigger, cheese as headache trigger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_2_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana_2-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_headaches" title="Cluster headaches">
       Cluster headaches
      </a>
      are relatively rare (1–3 in 10,000 people) and are more common in men than women. They present with sudden onset explosive pain around one eye and are accompanied by autonomic symptoms (tearing, runny nose and red eye).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Temporomandibular_joint_dysfunction" title="Temporomandibular joint dysfunction">
       Temporomandibular jaw pain
      </a>
      (chronic pain in the jaw joint), and
      <a href="/wiki/Cervicogenic_headache" title="Cervicogenic headache">
       cervicogenic headache
      </a>
      (headache caused by pain in muscles of the neck) are also possible diagnoses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smetana_22-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smetana-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For chronic, unexplained headaches, keeping a headache diary can be useful for tracking symptoms and identifying triggers, such as association with menstrual cycle, exercise and food. The National Migraine Center has free headache diaries to download at their website.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While mobile electronic diaries for smartphones are becoming increasingly common, a recent review found most are developed with a lack of evidence base and scientific expertise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_headaches">
       New headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: New headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      New headaches are more likely to be dangerous
      <a href="#Secondary_headaches">
       secondary headaches
      </a>
      . They can, however, simply be the first presentation of a chronic headache syndrome, like migraine or tension-type headaches.
     </p>
     <p>
      One recommended diagnostic approach is as follows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If any urgent
      <a href="#Red_flags">
       red flags
      </a>
      are present such as visual loss, new seizures, new weakness, new confusion, further workup with imaging and possibly a lumbar puncture should be done (see red flags section for more details). If the headache is sudden onset (thunderclap headache), a computed tomography test to look for a brain bleed (
      <a href="/wiki/Subarachnoid_hemorrhage" title="Subarachnoid hemorrhage">
       subarachnoid hemorrhage
      </a>
      ) should be done. If the CT scan does not show a bleed, a lumbar puncture should be done to look for blood in the CSF, as the CT scan can be falsely negative and subarachnoid hemorrhages can be fatal. If there are signs of infection such as fever, rash, or stiff neck, a lumbar puncture to look for meningitis should be considered. If there is jaw claudication and scalp tenderness in an older person, a temporal artery biopsy to look for temporal arteritis should be performed and immediate treatment should be started.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroimaging">
       Neuroimaging
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Neuroimaging">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_headaches_2">
       Old headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Old headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The US Headache Consortium has guidelines for neuroimaging of non-acute headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most old, chronic headaches do not require neuroimaging. If a person has the characteristic symptoms of a migraine, neuroimaging is not needed as it is very unlikely the person has an intracranial abnormality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHSfive_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AHSfive-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If the person has neurological findings, such as weakness, on exam, neuroimaging may be considered.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_headaches_2">
       New headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: New headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      All people who present with
      <a href="#Red_flags">
       red flags
      </a>
      indicating a dangerous secondary headache should receive neuroimaging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hainer_25-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hainer-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The best form of neuroimaging for these headaches is controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Non-contrast computerized tomography (CT) scan is usually the first step in head imaging as it is readily available in Emergency Departments and hospitals and is cheaper than MRI. Non-contrast CT is best for identifying an acute head bleed. Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) is best for brain tumors and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posterior_fossa_malformations%E2%80%93hemangiomas%E2%80%93arterial_anomalies%E2%80%93cardiac_defects%E2%80%93eye_abnormalities%E2%80%93sternal_cleft_and_supraumbilical_raphe_syndrome" title="Posterior fossa malformations–hemangiomas–arterial anomalies–cardiac defects–eye abnormalities–sternal cleft and supraumbilical raphe syndrome">
       problems in the posterior fossa
      </a>
      , or back of the brain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      MRI is more sensitive for identifying intracranial problems, however it can pick up brain abnormalities that are not relevant to the person's headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinch_6-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clinch-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The American College of Radiology recommends the following imaging tests for different specific situations:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Clinical Features
       </th>
       <th>
        Recommended neuroimaging test
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headache in immunocompromised people (cancer, HIV)
       </td>
       <td>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">
         MRI
        </a>
        of head with or without contrast
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headache in people older than 60 with suspected temporal arteritis
       </td>
       <td>
        MRI of head with or without contrast
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Headache with suspected meningitis
       </td>
       <td>
        CT or MRI without contrast
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Severe headache in pregnancy
       </td>
       <td>
        CT or MRI without contrast
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Severe unilateral headache caused by possible dissection of carotid and/or arterial arteries
       </td>
       <td>
        MRI of head with or without contrast,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_Resonance_Angiography" title="Magnetic Resonance Angiography">
         Magnetic Resonance Angiography
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Computed_tomography_angiography" title="Computed tomography angiography">
         Computed Tomography Angiography
        </a>
        of head and neck.
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Sudden onset headache or worst headache of life
       </td>
       <td>
        CT of head without contrast,
        <a href="/wiki/Computed_tomography_angiography" title="Computed tomography angiography">
         Computed Tomography Angiography
        </a>
        of head and neck with contrast,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_Resonance_Angiography" title="Magnetic Resonance Angiography">
         Magnetic Resonance Angiography
        </a>
        of head and neck with and without contrast, MRI of head without contrast
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lumbar_puncture">
       Lumbar puncture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lumbar puncture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_puncture" title="Lumbar puncture">
       lumbar puncture
      </a>
      is a procedure in which cerebral spinal fluid is removed from the spine with a needle. A lumbar puncture is necessary to look for infection or blood in the spinal fluid. A lumbar puncture can also evaluate the pressure in the spinal column, which can be useful for people with
      <a href="/wiki/Idiopathic_intracranial_hypertension" title="Idiopathic intracranial hypertension">
       idiopathic intracranial hypertension
      </a>
      (usually young, obese women who have increased intracranial pressure), or other causes of increased intracranial pressure. In most cases, a CT scan should be done first.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Classification">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Headaches are most thoroughly classified by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Headache_Society" title="International Headache Society">
       International Headache Society
      </a>
      's International Classification of Headache Disorders (ICHD), which published the second edition in 2004.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_ICHD2_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IHS_ICHD2-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The third edition of the International Headache Classification was published in 2013 in a beta version ahead of the final version.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This classification is accepted by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO">
       WHO
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlesen_et_al.20059.E2.80.9311_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlesen_et_al.20059.E2.80.9311-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other classification systems exist. One of the first published attempts was in 1951.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The US
      <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">
       National Institutes of Health
      </a>
      developed a classification system in 1962.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="ICHD-2">
       ICHD-2
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: ICHD-2">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_Headache_Disorders" title="International Classification of Headache Disorders">
       International Classification of Headache Disorders
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_Headache_Disorders" title="International Classification of Headache Disorders">
       International Classification of Headache Disorders
      </a>
      (ICHD) is an in-depth
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchical" title="Hierarchical">
       hierarchical
      </a>
      classification of headaches published by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Headache_Society" title="International Headache Society">
       International Headache Society
      </a>
      . It contains explicit (operational)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diagnostic_criteria" title="Diagnostic criteria">
       diagnostic criteria
      </a>
      for headache disorders. The first version of the classification, ICHD-1, was published in 1988. The current revision, ICHD-2, was published in 2004.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The classification uses numeric codes. The top, one-digit diagnostic level includes 14 headache groups. The first four of these are classified as primary headaches, groups 5-12 as secondary headaches,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_cranium" title="Human cranium">
       cranial
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Neuralgia" title="Neuralgia">
       neuralgia
      </a>
      , central and primary facial pain and other headaches for the last two groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The ICHD-2 classification defines
      <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">
       migraines
      </a>
      , tension-types headaches, cluster headache and other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigeminal" title="Trigeminal">
       trigeminal
      </a>
      autonomic headache as the main types of primary headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_ICHD2_33-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IHS_ICHD2-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also, according to the same classification, stabbing headaches and headaches due to
      <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">
       cough
      </a>
      , exertion and sexual activity (
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_headache" title="Sexual headache">
       sexual headache
      </a>
      ) are classified as primary headaches. The daily-persistent headaches along with the hypnic headache and thunderclap headaches are considered primary headaches as well.
     </p>
     <p>
      Secondary headaches are classified based on their etiology and not on their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptoms" title="Symptoms">
       symptoms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_ICHD2_33-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-IHS_ICHD2-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the ICHD-2 classification, the main types of secondary headaches include those that are due to head or neck trauma such as
      <a href="/wiki/Whiplash_(medicine)" title="Whiplash (medicine)">
       whiplash injury
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intracranial_hematoma" title="Intracranial hematoma">
       intracranial hematoma
      </a>
      , post
      <a href="/wiki/Craniotomy" title="Craniotomy">
       craniotomy
      </a>
      or other head or neck injury. Headaches caused by cranial or cervical vascular disorders such as
      <a href="/wiki/Stroke#Ischemic_stroke" title="Stroke">
       ischemic stroke
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Transient_ischemic_attack" title="Transient ischemic attack">
       transient ischemic attack
      </a>
      , non-traumatic intracranial hemorrhage,
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_arteriovenous_malformation" title="Cerebral arteriovenous malformation">
       vascular malformations
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Arteritis" title="Arteritis">
       arteritis
      </a>
      are also defined as secondary headaches. This type of headaches may also be caused by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerebral_venous_thrombosis" title="Cerebral venous thrombosis">
       cerebral venous thrombosis
      </a>
      or different intracranial vascular disorders. Other secondary headaches are those due to intracranial disorders that are not vascular such as low or high pressure of the cerebrospinal fluid pressure, non-infectious inflammatory disease, intracranial neoplasm,
      <a href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">
       epileptic seizure
      </a>
      or other types of disorders or diseases that are intracranial but that are not associated with the vasculature of the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      . ICHD-2 classifies headaches that are caused by the ingestion of a certain substance or by its withdrawal as secondary headaches as well. This type of headache may result from the overuse of some medications or by exposure to some substances.
      <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">
       HIV
      </a>
      /
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">
       AIDS
      </a>
      , intracranial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infections" title="Infections">
       infections
      </a>
      and systemic infections may also cause secondary headaches. The ICHD-2 system of classification includes the headaches associated with homeostasis disorders in the category of secondary headaches. This means that headaches caused by
      <a href="/wiki/Dialysis" title="Dialysis">
       dialysis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">
       high blood pressure
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">
       hypothyroidism
      </a>
      , and cephalalgia and even
      <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">
       fasting
      </a>
      are considered secondary headaches. Secondary headaches, according to the same classification system, can also be due to the injury of any of the facial structures including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teeth" title="Teeth">
       teeth
      </a>
      , jaws, or
      <a href="/wiki/Temporomandibular_joint" title="Temporomandibular joint">
       temporomandibular joint
      </a>
      . Headaches caused by psychiatric disorders such as
      <a href="/wiki/Somatization" title="Somatization">
       somatization
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychotic_disorders" title="Psychotic disorders">
       psychotic disorders
      </a>
      are also classified as secondary headaches.
     </p>
     <p>
      The ICHD-2 classification puts cranial neuralgias and other types of
      <a href="/wiki/Neuralgia" title="Neuralgia">
       neuralgia
      </a>
      in a different category. According to this system, there are 19 types of neuralgias and headaches due to different central causes of facial pain. Moreover, the ICHD-2 includes a category that contains all the headaches that cannot be classified.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the ICHD-2 is the most complete headache classification there is and it includes frequency in the diagnostic criteria of some types of headaches (primarily primary headaches), it does not specifically code frequency or severity which are left at the discretion of the examiner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_ICHD2_33-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-IHS_ICHD2-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="NIH">
       NIH
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: NIH">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/NIH_classification_of_headaches" title="NIH classification of headaches">
       NIH classification of headaches
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The NIH classification consists of brief definitions of a limited number of headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevin_et_al.200860_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevin_et_al.200860-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The NIH system of classification is more succinct and only describes five categories of headaches. In this case, primary headaches are those that do not show organic or structural
      <a href="/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">
       etiology
      </a>
      . According to this classification, headaches can only be vascular,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myogenic" title="Myogenic">
       myogenic
      </a>
      , cervicogenic, traction and inflammatory.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Management">
       Management
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Management">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image-Lottie_Collins_sings_and_dances_to_the_tunes_of_Ta-Ra-Ra_Boom-de-ay_in_a_Bromo-Seltzer_ad.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="250" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Image-Lottie_Collins_sings_and_dances_to_the_tunes_of_Ta-Ra-Ra_Boom-de-ay_in_a_Bromo-Seltzer_ad.jpg/220px-Image-Lottie_Collins_sings_and_dances_to_the_tunes_of_Ta-Ra-Ra_Boom-de-ay_in_a_Bromo-Seltzer_ad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Image-Lottie_Collins_sings_and_dances_to_the_tunes_of_Ta-Ra-Ra_Boom-de-ay_in_a_Bromo-Seltzer_ad.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image-Lottie_Collins_sings_and_dances_to_the_tunes_of_Ta-Ra-Ra_Boom-de-ay_in_a_Bromo-Seltzer_ad.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An old advertisement for a headache medicine.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Management_of_chronic_headaches" title="Management of chronic headaches">
       Management of chronic headaches
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Primary headache syndromes have many different possible treatments. In those with chronic headaches the long term use of opioids appears to result in greater harm than benefit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Migraines">
       Migraines
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Migraines">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migraines" title="Migraines">
       Migraine
      </a>
      can be somewhat improved by lifestyle changes, but most people require medicines to control their symptoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Medications are either to prevent getting migraines, or to reduce symptoms once a migraine starts.
     </p>
     <p>
      Preventive medications are generally recommended when people have more than four attacks of migraine per month, headaches last longer than 12 hours or the headaches are very disabling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTD_Migraine_Tx_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UTD_Migraine_Tx-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Possible therapies include beta blockers, antidepressants, anticonvulsants and NSAIDs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTD_Migraine_Tx_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-UTD_Migraine_Tx-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The type of preventive medicine is usually chosen based on the other symptoms the person has. For example, if the person also has depression, an antidepressant is a good choice.
     </p>
     <p>
      Abortive therapies for migraines may be oral, if the migraine is mild to moderate, or may require stronger medicine given intravenously or intramuscularly. Mild to moderate headaches should first be treated with NSAIDs, like ibuprofen, and/or acetaminophen. If accompanied by nausea or vomiting, an antiemitic such as metoclopramide (Reglan) can be given orally or rectally. Moderate to severe attacks should be treated first with an oral
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triptans" title="Triptans">
       triptan
      </a>
      , a medication which mimics serotonin (an agonist) and causes mild vasoconstriction. If accompanied by nausea and vomiting, parenteral (through a needle in the skin) triptans and antiemetics can be given.
     </p>
     <p>
      Several complementary and alternative strategies can help with migraines. The American Academy of Neurology guidelines for migraine treatment in 2000 stated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relaxation_training" title="Relaxation training">
       relaxation training
      </a>
      , electromyographic feedback and
      <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">
       cognitive behavioral therapy
      </a>
      may be considered for migraine treatment, along with medications.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tension-type_headaches">
       Tension-type headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tension-type headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension-type_headache" title="Tension-type headache">
       Tension-type headaches
      </a>
      can usually be managed with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDs" title="NSAIDs">
       NSAIDs
      </a>
      (ibuprofen, naproxen),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetaminophen" title="Acetaminophen">
       acetaminophen
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">
       aspirin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triptans" title="Triptans">
       Triptans
      </a>
      are not helpful in tension-type headaches unless the person also has migraines. For chronic tension type headaches,
      <a href="/wiki/Amitriptyline" title="Amitriptyline">
       amitriptyline
      </a>
      is the only medication proven to help.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UpToDate_Tension_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UpToDate_Tension-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amitryptiline_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Amitryptiline-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Amitriptyline is a medication which treats depression and also independently treats pain. It works by blocking the reuptake of serotonin and norepinephrine, and also reduces muscle tenderness by a separate mechanism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UpToDate_Tension_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-UpToDate_Tension-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies evaluating acupuncture for tension-type headaches have been mixed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Overall, they show that acupuncture is probably not helpful for tension-type headaches.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cluster_headaches">
       Cluster headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cluster headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Abortive therapy for cluster headaches includes subcutaneous sumatriptan (injected under the skin) and triptan nasal sprays. High flow oxygen therapy also helps with relief.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For people with extended periods of cluster headaches, preventive therapy can be necessary. Verapamil is recommended as first line treatment. Lithium can also be useful. For people with shorter bouts, a short course of prednisone (10 days) can be helpful. Ergotamine is useful if given 1–2 hours before an attack.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goadsby_5-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goadsby-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      See
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_headaches" title="Cluster headaches">
       cluster headaches
      </a>
      for more detailed information.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary_headaches_2">
       Secondary headaches
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Secondary headaches">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Treatment of secondary headaches involves treating the underlying cause. For example, a person with meningitis will require antibiotics. A person with a
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">
       brain tumor
      </a>
      may require surgery,
      <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">
       chemotherapy
      </a>
      and/or brain radiation.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuromodulation">
       Neuromodulation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Neuromodulation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Peripheral neuromodulation has tentative benefits in primary headaches including cluster headaches and chronic migraine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed2013_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed2013-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      How it may work is still being looked into.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed2013_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed2013-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">
       Epidemiology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Approximately 64–77% of people have a headache at some point in their lives. During each year, on average, 46–53% of people have headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manzoni_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Manzoni-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stovner_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stovner-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of these headaches are not dangerous. Only approximately 1–5% of people who seek emergency treatment for headaches have a serious underlying cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More than 90% of headaches are primary headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-D-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of these primary headaches are tension headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stovner_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stovner-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most people with tension headaches have "episodic" tension headaches that come and go. Only 3.3% of adults have chronic tension headaches, with headaches for more than 15 days in a month.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stovner_53-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stovner-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Approximately 12–18% of people in the world have migraines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stovner_53-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stovner-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      More women than men experience migraines. In Europe and North America, 5–9% of men experience migraines, while 12–25% of women experience migraines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manzoni_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Manzoni-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Cluster headaches are very rare. They affect only 1–3 per thousand people in the world. Cluster headaches affect approximately three times as many men as women.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stovner_53-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stovner-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="800" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png/220px-Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png/330px-Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png/440px-Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cruikshank_-_The_Head_Ache.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An 1819 caricature by
        <a href="/wiki/George_Cruikshank" title="George Cruikshank">
         George Cruikshank
        </a>
        depicting a headache.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first recorded classification system that resembles the modern ones was published by
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Willis" title="Thomas Willis">
       Thomas Willis
      </a>
      , in
      <i>
       De Cephalalgia
      </i>
      in 1672. In 1787
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_Baur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Christian Baur (page does not exist)">
       Christian Baur
      </a>
      generally divided headaches into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idiopathic" title="Idiopathic">
       idiopathic
      </a>
      (primary headaches) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptomatic" title="Symptomatic">
       symptomatic
      </a>
      (secondary ones), and defined 84 categories.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevin_et_al.200860_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevin_et_al.200860-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another name for headache is "cephalalgia", which is a historic term.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Children">
       Children
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Headache&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Children">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In general, children suffer from the same types of headaches as adults do, but their symptoms may be slightly different. The diagnostic approach to headache in children is similar to that of adults. However, young children may not be able to verbalize pain well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If a young child is fussy, they may have a headache.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Approximately 1% of Emergency Department visits for children are for headache.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kan_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kan-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of these headaches are not dangerous. The most common type of headache seen in pediatric Emergency Rooms is headache caused by a cold (28.5%). Other headaches diagnosed in the Emergency Department include post-traumatic headache (20%), headache related to a problem with a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventriculoperitoneal_shunt" title="Ventriculoperitoneal shunt">
       ventriculoperitoneal shunt
      </a>
      (a device put into the brain to remove excess
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">
       CSF
      </a>
      and reduce pressure in the brain) (11.5%) and migraine (8.5%).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kan_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kan-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common serious headaches found in children include brain bleeds (
      <a href="/wiki/Subdural_hematoma" title="Subdural hematoma">
       subdural hematoma
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Epidural_hematoma" title="Epidural hematoma">
       epidural hematoma
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_abscess" title="Brain abscess">
       brain abscesses
      </a>
      , meningitis and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventriculoperitoneal_shunt" title="Ventriculoperitoneal shunt">
       ventriculoperitoneal shunt
      </a>
      malfunction. Only 4–6.9% of kids with a headache have a serious cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Just as in adults, most headaches are benign, but when head pain is accompanied with other symptoms such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speech_problems" title="Speech problems">
       speech problems
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Muscle_weakness" title="Muscle weakness">
       muscle weakness
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_vision" title="Loss of vision">
       loss of vision
      </a>
      , a more serious underlying cause may exist:
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">
       hydrocephalus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">
       meningitis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">
       encephalitis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Abscess" title="Abscess">
       abscess
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" title="Hemorrhage">
       hemorrhage
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">
       tumor
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_clot" title="Blood clot">
       blood clots
      </a>
      , or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_trauma" title="Head trauma">
       head trauma
      </a>
      . In these cases, the headache evaluation may include CT scan or MRI in order to look for possible structural disorders of the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If a child with a recurrent headache has a normal physical exam, neuroimaging is not recommended. Guidelines state children with abnormal neurologic exams, confusion, seizures and recent onset of worst headache of life, change in headache type or anything suggesting neurologic problems should receive neuroimaging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_57-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When children complain of headaches, many parents are concerned about a
      <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">
       brain tumor
      </a>
      . Generally, headaches caused by brain masses are incapacitating and accompanied by vomiting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_57-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      One study found characteristics associated with brain tumor in children are: headache for greater than 6 months, headache related to sleep, vomiting, confusion, no visual symptoms, no family history of migraine and abnormal
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurologic_exam" title="Neurologic exam">
       neurologic exam
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some measures can help prevent headaches in children. Drinking plenty of water throughout the day, avoiding caffeine, getting enough and regular sleep, eating balanced meals at the proper times, and reducing stress and excess of activities may prevent headaches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Treatments for children are similar to those for adults, however certain medications such as narcotics should not be given to children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_57-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schultz-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Children who have headaches will not necessarily have headaches as adults. In one study of 100 children with headache, eight years later 44% of those with tension headache and 28% of those with migraines were headache free.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In another study of people with chronic daily headache, 75% did not have chronic daily headaches two years later, and 88% did not have chronic daily headaches eight years later.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
